reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Simple model that performs 2D Average Pooling.
    """
    def __init__(self, kernel_size: int, stride: int = None, padding: int = 0):
        """
        Initializes the Average Pooling layer.

        Args:
            kernel_size (int): Size of the pooling window.
            stride (int, optional): Stride of the pooling operation. Defaults to None (same as kernel_size).
            padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input tensor. Defaults to 0.
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.avg_pool = nn.AvgPool2d(kernel_size=kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Applies 2D Average Pooling to the input tensor.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, channels, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor with Average Pooling applied.
        """
        return self.avg_pool(x)

batch_size = 16
channels = 64
height = 2048
width = 2048
kernel_size = 11

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, channels, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [kernel_size]

We need to write a custom CUDA kernel for 2D average pooling. This is a fairly standard operation but the input size is large (16, 64, 2048, 2048) with kernel_size=11. PyTorch's built-in AvgPool2d is already quite optimized, but we can potentially do better with a custom kernel that: 1. Uses shared memory to cache input tiles 2. Processes multiple pixels per thread 3. Uses vectorized loads (float4) where possible 4. Is carefully tuned for the specific problem dimensions Given the large image size (2048x2048) and batch*channels = 1024, we have ~4 billion input elements. Wait, each thread has 121 floats. To store them to shmem, thread i stores to indices i, i+32, i+64, ... in shared memory. This means: Thread 0: banks 0, 0, 0, ... (since 32 floats span 32 banks, address 0 is bank 0, 32 is bank 0, 64 is bank 0, etc.) Actually, shared memory banks: 32 banks, 4 bytes each. Address s has bank = s / 4 % 32. Address 0: bank 0 Address 32*4=128: bank 0 Address 64*4=256: bank 0 So all threads accessing the same bank but different addresses would be... wait, thread i stores to i*4, (i+32)*4, (i+64)*4. Bank for i*4 is i%32. For (i+32)*4 is (i+32)%32 = i%32. So each thread stores to the SAME bank for all its elements! But at different times, so no conflict (since it's the same thread). Bank conflicts only happen when multiple threads access the same bank in the same instruction. Here, for a single store instruction, all 32 threads are storing. Thread i stores to address i*4 (bank i). That's 32 different banks! Perfect! No bank conflict for the first element. For the second element: thread i stores to (i+32)*4 (bank i). Again, 32 different banks! Because 32 % 32 = 0. So address (i+32)*4 ≡ i*4 (mod 128). The bank is (i+32)%32 = i%32. And all threads store to distinct banks (thread 0: bank 0, thread 1: bank 1, etc.). So there are NO bank conflicts! This is because the stride is a multiple of 32 floats, which is 128 bytes = the number of banks * bank width. So each "column" is bank-conflict-free. We can keep the data in SHARED MEMORY but the threads that LOAD from global are the SAME threads that READ from shmem later. But the pattern is different. Thread t loads 121 floats scattered across the tile. Thread t later reads 121 floats forming its 11x11 block. So if we store to shmem directly in the loaded pattern (which is thread t -> shmem[t + k*64]), then thread t later must read its 11x11 block from those locations. Address for pixel (ty, tx), element (ky, kx) within block: Tile base in input: (tile_oh*11 + ty*11 + ky) * width + tile_ow*11 + tx*11 + kx. Thread assigned to (ty, tx) needs this value. In shmem, the data is laid out in the same order as input: row-major. Specifically, shmem_idx = (tile_ty*11 + ky) * tile_width + tile_tx*11 + kx. Where tile_width = TILE_X * 11 = 352. So thread (ty,tx) reads shmem at indices: (ty*11 + ky)*352 + tx*11 + kx. As long as consecutive threads (in a warp) access consecutive or well-distributed banks, and we established that for a warp handling the same ty, tx=0..31, the accesses are bank-conflict-free for individual float loads. So we don't need float4 for shmem. We can use 121 individual float loads/stores. But 121 shmem stores per thread (from global load) and 121 shmem loads per thread (for sum). That's 242 shmem instructions. Plus 31 global instructions. Instruction count is high but these are simple, non-divergent instructions. For a memory-bound kernel, the bottleneck is global memory, not these instructions. The GPU can execute them while waiting for global memory. But can we avoid shmem altogether and just load directly into registers, then reduce via warp shuffles? No, because the mapping is not a simple transpose. WAIT! A better idea. What if we DON'T use shared memory, but instead use the trick that for NON-OVERLAPPING blocks, we can accumulate by having each thread process MULTIPLE positions using coalesced loads? For example, with 64 threads in a block handling 2x32 output pixels: Instead of having thread (ty,tx) sum over 11x11 block for pixel (ty,tx), we can restructure the computation. Actually, there is a very elegant approach using a warp-level matrix-like reduction. Consider a warp of 32 threads processing a 1x32 output tile. Each thread handles one output pixel. The input region is 11x352 floats. We load this 11x352 region in a coalesced way. But we don't need to store it all to shmem. We can accumulate the sum incrementally. For each row in 0..10: For each chunk of 32 floats in 0..10 within the row: Load 32 floats coalesced into a warp (one float per thread). Then use warp shuffle to send the float to the thread that needs it. Specifically, for row r and output pixel tx, the thread needs input[r*width + tx*11 + x] for x=0..10. If we iterate x=0..10: At step x, all threads load input[r*width + base_ow*11 + tx*11 + x] = input[r*width + (base_ow+tx)*11 + x]. Thread tx loads this. But this is exactly what we want! Thread tx loads its own value. But as we established, the global loads are strided by 11 (44 bytes) across threads. Terrible coalescing. What if at step x we have threads load a CONTIGUOUS chunk of 32 floats, then shuffle? For step x, the values needed by the 32 output pixels are: V[tx] = input[r*width + base_ow*11 + tx*11 + x]. These are at offsets: 0, 11, 22, ..., 341 within the row. If we have threads load a contiguous region: Thread i loads input[r*width + base_ow*11 + i] for i=0..31. Then we need to get V[tx] = input[r*width + base_ow*11 + tx*11 + x] to thread tx. We can do this via shuffle? V[tx] is at index tx*11+x. We can have thread tx read from register of thread (tx*11+x) if that thread has it. But each thread only has one value. So we'd need thread j to pass its value to thread tx where j = tx*11 + x. For fixed x, j = tx*11 + x. For tx=0..31, j ranges over 32 distinct values (since 11 is coprime to 32). Each thread tx needs one value from thread j. So we can do a __shfl_sync! Specifically: // Step x: thread i loads global[i] // Then thread tx gets value from lane (tx*11 + x) % 32? Wait, no. Thread j has loaded global[base + j]. Thread tx needs global[base + tx*11 + x]. If j = tx*11 + x, then thread tx needs the value from thread j. But j depends on tx. For a fixed x, the mapping tx -> j is a permutation of {0..31} (because gcd(11,32)=1). The concurrency from 22 blocks per SM should hide latency well. One more consideration: PyTorch likely uses cuDNN for AvgPool2d when possible. Actually, PyTorch has a native CUDA implementation. cuDNN pooling might be highly optimized. Our kernel has 0 bank conflicts, almost perfect coalescing, no bounds checks, tiny code. It should be very competitive. Let's write the CUDA kernel code. Since the dimensions are compile-time constants in practice (we know k=11, s=11, p=0), but we should write it generally just in case. Actually, the reference always uses kernel_size=11 and default stride=11, padding=0. We can specialize the kernel for kernel_size=11 to loop unroll completely. Using a template parameter `int K` and instantiating K=11 would allow the compiler to unroll loops. In the CUDA code: ```cuda template<int K> __global__ void avgpool2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int OH, int OW ) { constexpr int OUT_TILE_H = 3; constexpr int OUT_TILE_W = 31; constexpr int IN_TILE_H = OUT_TILE_H * K; // 33 constexpr int IN_TILE_W = OUT_TILE_W * K; // 341 constexpr int BLOCK = OUT_TILE_H * OUT_TILE_W; // 93 __shared__ float shmem[OUT_TILE_H * IN_TILE_W]; // 3*341 int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = tx / OUT_TILE_W; // 0..2 int tc = tx % OUT_TILE_W; // 0..30 int ow_tile = blockIdx.x; int oh_tile = blockIdx.y; int nc = blockIdx.z; int oh = oh_tile * OUT_TILE_H + ty; int ow = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W + tc; const float* in_slice = input + ((size_t)nc * H * W); float* out_slice = output + ((size_t)nc * OH * OW); int in_row_base = oh_tile * OUT_TILE_H * K; int in_col_base = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W * K; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { // Each group of OUT_TILE_W threads loads one row int group = ty; // 0,1,2 int row_in_group = k; int in_row = in_row_base + group * K + row_in_group; // Thread tc within group loads column tc + m*31 #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < OUT_TILE_W; ++m) { int col = tc + m * OUT_TILE_W; // Wait, OUT_TILE_W=31. We need 11 iterations to cover 341 columns. // 31*11 = 341. For group g: in_row = in_row_base + g*K + k. Thread tc in the block loads columns: tc, tc+31, tc+62, ..., tc+310. That's a separate loop of 11 loads. Fine. And shmem only needs to hold K rows (1 per group) because we overwrite each iteration. Actually, we only need shmem[3][341] as before. Each iteration overwrites it. Consider the code: ```cuda float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ky = 0; ky < K; ++ky) { int in_row = in_row_base + ty * K + ky; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) { int col = tc + m * OUT_TILE_W; shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + col] = in_slice[in_row * W + in_col_base + col]; } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int kx = 0; kx < K; ++kx) { sum += shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + tc * K + kx]; } __syncthreads(); } out_slice[oh * OW + ow] = sum * (1.0f / (float)(K * K)); ``` Wait, but ty = tx / OUT_TILE_W. Let's write the kernel: ```cuda template <int K> __global__ void avgpool2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int OH, int OW ) { constexpr int OUT_TILE_W = 31; constexpr int IN_TILE_W = OUT_TILE_W * K; constexpr int INV_KK = K * K; __shared__ float shmem[3 * IN_TILE_W]; // 3 groups, each IN_TILE_W floats int tc = threadIdx.x; // 0..30 int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..2 int ow_tile = blockIdx.x; int oh_tile = blockIdx.y; int nc = blockIdx.z; const float* in_slice = input + ((size_t)nc * H * W); float* out_slice = output + ((size_t)nc * OH * OW); int in_row_base = oh_tile * 3 * K; int in_col_base = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W * K; int oh = oh_tile * 3 + ty; int ow = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W + tc; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ky = 0; ky < K; ++ky) { int in_row = in_row_base + ty * K + ky; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) { int col = tc + m * OUT_TILE_W; shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + col] = in_slice[in_row * W + in_col_base + col]; } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int kx = 0; kx < K; ++kx) { sum += shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + tc * K + kx]; } __syncthreads(); } out_slice[oh * OW + ow] = sum * (1.0f / (float)(INV_KK)); } ``` Wait, we have a subtle but critical issue. In the loading loop, ALL threads in the block load into shmem. Thread (ty, tc) writes to `shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + col]`. After sync, in the accumulation loop, thread (ty, tc) reads from `shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + tc*K + kx]`. BUT: between iterations `ky`, we overwrite `shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + ...]`. In iteration ky=0, we load row 0 for group ty. Then sum += shmem[ty*IW + tc*K + 0..K-1]. In iteration ky=1, we load row 1 for group ty, overwriting the data from row 0. Then sum += shmem[ty*IW + tc*K + 0..K-1] (which now contains row 1 data). This is CORRECT! Because we are accumulating the sum row by row. We don't need all rows in shmem at once. We load one row, accumulate it, then load the next. This is actually a streaming accumulation through shared memory! Brilliant. But wait, the `__syncthreads()` between load and accumulate is essential to ensure all threads have finished writing before any thread reads. And `__syncthreads()` after accumulate is to ensure all threads have finished reading before the next iteration overwrites. Actually, we DO need the second sync. Because some threads might finish accumulation quickly and start loading the next iteration, overwriting data that slow threads are still reading. So two `__syncthreads()` per iteration. With K=11, that's 22 syncs per block. `__syncthreads()` is a barrier. In a 93-thread block (3 warps), it's cheap. Cost: maybe 10-20 cycles. 22 * 20 = 440 cycles = ~0.3 microseconds at 1.4 GHz. Negligible compared to memory time. Could we avoid the second sync by having the loads and accumulates use separate parts of shared memory in a double-buffered fashion? Since we have 3*341 shmem, we could use two buffers of 3*341. But that's double the memory. Or we can overlap: while loading into buffer 1, accumulate from buffer 0. Ping-pong. But 3*341 = 4092 bytes. Double is 8KB. Still tiny. But we have only one `shmem` array. Actually, we can split it: `shmem[2][3][341]`. Iteration ky: buffer = ky % 2. Load into buffer 1, accumulate from buffer 0 (if ky > 0). But then we still need syncs between load and accumulate. However, we can remove the accumulate-after-load sync if we careful... No, we always need a sync between write and subsequent read. Wait, actually: Iteration ky: Threads write to buffer b = ky & 1. __syncthreads(); Threads read from buffer b = ky & 1 and accumulate. // Now threads are done writing previous iteration's buffer and reading current. // But we need to ensure all reads from buffer b are done before next iteration's // threads start writing to buffer b again. // Next iteration ky+1 writes to buffer 1-b. So no conflict! // Threads can proceed to next iteration immediately after accumulation! // We only need ONE __syncthreads() per iteration: after the load, before the accumulate. // Wait, actually the load is done by all threads. We need the load to be complete // before any thread reads. That's one sync. // For the next iteration, we write to the OTHER buffer, so no conflict. // We don't need a sync after accumulation! This is true! Double buffering with 2 buffers removes one sync per iteration. So we go from 22 syncs to 11 syncs. This might give a tiny speedup. Let's implement double buffering: ```cuda __shared__ float shmem[2][3 * IN_TILE_W]; // or shmem[2][3][IN_TILE_W] // ... int buf = ky & 1; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) { int col = tc + m * OUT_TILE_W; shmem[buf * (3 * IN_TILE_W) + ty * IN_TILE_W + col] = in_slice[in_row * W + in_col_base + col]; } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int kx = 0; kx < K; ++kx) { sum += shmem[buf * (3 * IN_TILE_W) + ty * IN_TILE_W + tc * K + kx]; } // No __syncthreads() here! ``` But wait, is this safe? Consider slow threads in warp 2 still reading from `shmem[buf]`. Fast threads in warp 0 might finish their accumulation and move to the next iteration ky+1. ky+1 writes to `shmem[1-buf]`. So it does NOT overwrite `shmem[buf]`. So slow threads reading `shmem[buf]` are safe. No need for a second sync! This is correct. This is a beautiful optimization. Total shmem: 2 * 3 * 341 * 4 = 8184 bytes (8KB). Still very small. Blocks per SM: up to 32 (thread limited to 22). Double buffer doesn't hurt occupancy. Now let's verify one more time: For ky=0, buf=0. Write to shmem[0][...]. Sync. Read from shmem[0][...]. Sum. For ky=1, buf=1. Write to shmem[1][...]. Sync. Read from shmem[1][...]. Sum. The write to shmem[1] by fast threads might happen while slow threads are still reading shmem[0]. That's fine. No race. The write to shmem[0] at ky=2: buf=0 again. Fast threads write to shmem[0] while slow threads might still be reading shmem[1] (if they are very slow). Actually, at ky=2, we write to buf=0. Some threads might still be at ky=1 reading buf=1. But we are writing to buf=0. Different buffers. Safe. BUT, what if some threads are at ky=0 reading buf=0? That's impossible because the program is SIMT. All threads in a warp execute the same instruction. Divergence only happens with branches. Here, the loop is `#pragma unroll`, so all warps execute ky=0 then ky=1 etc. But different warps within a block can be at different iterations. Warp 0 at ky=2 writing to buf=0, while warp 2 at ky=1 reading buf=1. Safe. Warp 0 at ky=2 writing to buf=0, while warp 2 at ky=0? No, warp 2 cannot be 2 iterations behind if they started together. Due to barriers at __syncthreads(), all warps in the block must reach the sync before any can proceed. Wait! The kernel loop: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int ky = 0; ky < K; ++ky) { int in_row = in_row_base + ty * K + ky; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) { int col = tc + m * OUT_TILE_W; shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + col] = in_slice[in_row * W + in_col_base + col]; } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int kx = 0; kx < K; ++kx) { sum += shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + tc * K + kx]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` The compiler might not fully unroll the outer loop if K is not a compile-time constant in the template instantiation. We instantiate `<11>`, so it should unroll. Let's make sure to instantiate the template in `cpp_sources`. In the host wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor avgpool2d_forward(torch::Tensor input, int kernel_size, int stride, int padding) { int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int OH = (H + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1; int OW = (W + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1; auto output = torch::empty({N, C, OH, OW}, input.options()); if (kernel_size == 11 && stride == 11 && padding == 0 && OH % 3 == 0 && OW % 31 == 0) { dim3 block(31, 3); dim3 grid(OW / 31, OH / 3, N * C); avgpool2d_kernel<11><<<grid, block>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, OH, OW ); } else { // fallback not expected for main benchmark output = torch::nn::functional::avg_pool2d( input, torch::nn::functional::AvgPool2dFuncOptions(kernel_size) .stride(stride).padding(padding) ); } return output; } ``` Wait, using `torch::nn::functional::avg_pool2d` in C++ might require headers. It's easier to do the fallback in Python: ```python if self.kernel_size == self.stride and self.padding == 0 and x.size(2) % (3*self.stride) ... hmm. ``` Actually, it's cleaner to have the fallback in C++ too. But `torch::nn::functional` is available in libtorch. When we compile with `load_inline`, PyTorch C++ API is available. However, to be safe, I can just do the fallback in Python without C++. ky < K; ++ky) {\n int in_row = in_row_base + ty * K + ky;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) {\n int col = tc + m * OUT_TILE_W;\n shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + col] = in_slice[in_row * W + in_col_base + col];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int kx = 0; // 341 for K=11\n constexpr int KK = K * K; // 121 for K=11\n\n __shared__ float shmem[3 * IN_TILE_W]; // 3 * 341 floats (~4KB)\n\n int tc = threadIdx.x; // 0..30\n int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..2\n\n int ow_tile = blockIdx.x;\n int oh_tile = blockIdx.y;\n int nc = blockIdx.z;\n\n const float* in_slice = input + ((size_t)nc * H * W);\n float* out_slice = output + ((size_t)nc * OH * OW);\n\n int in_row_base = oh_tile * 3 * K;\n int in_col_base = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W * K;\n\n float sum = 0.0f;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int ky = 0; ky < K; ++ky) {\n int in_row = in_row_base + ty * K + ky;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) {\n int col = tc + m * OUT_TILE_W;\n shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + col] = in_slice[in_row * W + in_col_base + col];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int kx = 0; kx < K; ++kx) {\n sum += shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + tc * K + kx];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n\n int oh = oh_tile * 3 + ty;\n int ow = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W + tc;\n out_slice[oh * OW + ow] = sum * (1.0f / static_cast<float>(KK));\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor avgpool2d_forward(torch::Tensor input, int kernel_size, int stride, int padding) {\n int N = input.size(0);\n int C = input.size(1);\n int H = input.size(2);\n int W = input.size(3);\n int OH = (H + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1;\n int OW = (W + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1;\n\n auto output = torch::empty({N, C, OH, OW}, input.options());\n\n bool fast = (kernel_size == 11 && stride == 11 && padding == 0 &&\n OH % 3 == 0 && OW % 31 == 0);\n\n if (fast) {\n dim3 block(31, 3);\n dim3 grid(OW / 31, OH / 3, N * C);\n avgpool2d_kernel<11><<<grid, block>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C, H, W, OH, OW\n );\n } else {\n output = at::avg_pool2d(input, {kernel_size, kernel_size},\n {stride, stride}, {padding, padding},\n false, true);\n }\n return output;\n}\n'''\n\nmodule = load_inline(\n name='avgpool2d_cuda_v2',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['avgpool2d_forward'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'],\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, kernel_size: int, stride: int = None, padding: int = 0):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.kernel_size = kernel_size\n self.stride = stride if stride is not None else kernel_size\n self.padding = padding\n\n def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:\n return module.avgpool2d_forward(x, self.kernel_size, self.stride, self.padding)\n" }

Its contributing input rows are `in_row_start + ky` for `ky=0..K-1`, where `in_row_start = oh * K` (since stride=K). `in_row = oh*K + ky = (oh_tile*3 + ty)*K + ky = oh_tile*3*K + ty*K + ky`. This matches. For a given output column `ow = ow_tile*OUT_TILE_W + tc`. Its contributing input columns are `in_col_start + kx` for `kx=0..K-1`, where `in_col_start = ow * K`. `in_col = (ow_tile*OUT_TILE_W + tc)*K + kx = ow_tile*OUT_TILE_W*K + tc*K + kx = in_col_base + tc*K + kx`. The shared memory stores `shmem[ty*IN_TILE_W + col]` where `col` ranges 0..341. We want to access `col = tc*K + kx` later. Did we store that? For `m=0..K-1`, `col = tc + m*31`. So for a thread with `tc=0`, it stores columns `0, 31, 62, ..., 310`. For `tc=1`, it stores `1, 32, 63, ..., 311`. ... For `tc=30`, it stores `30, 61, 92, ..., 340`. This stores ALL columns `0..340` exactly once, because it's a strided layout: shmem index = `tc + m*31` corresponds to column `col`. So when we access `shmem[ty*IN_TILE_W + tc*K + kx]`, we are using `tc*K + kx` as the index. Is `tc*K + kx` equal to the stored index? For `tc=0`, we want `0, 1, ..., 10`. Who stored these? - `col=0`: stored by `tc=0, m=0`. - `col=1`: stored by `tc=1, m=0`. - ... - `col=10`: stored by `tc=10, m=0`. But thread `tc=0` later reads `shmem[ty*IN_TILE_W + 0*11 + kx]`, i.e., indices `0..10`. Index 0 was stored by `tc=0, m=0`. Index 1 was stored by `tc=1, m=0`. Since shared memory is shared across the block, thread `tc=0` can read what thread `tc=1` wrote. That's fine after `__syncthreads()`. So the mapping is: Stored index `col` corresponds to logical input column offset `col` (0 to 340). The thread `tc` later reads indices `tc*K + kx`. This maps to input columns `tc*K .. tc*K+K-1`. Since `tc` ranges 0..30, the columns read are `0..340`. Correct. Is there a bank conflict when reading? Shared memory is arranged as banks of 4 bytes on newer GPUs (Hopper). Each float is 4 bytes. Bank index = address / 4 % 32. Thread `tc` reads `shmem[ty*IN_TILE_W + tc*K + kx]` for a fixed `kx` across warp? No, `kx` is unrolled loop. For `kx=0`: thread `tc` reads address `base + tc*11`. Bank = `(base/4 + tc*11) % 32`. Since `11` is odd, `tc*11 % 32` is a permutation of `0..31` for `tc=0..31` (almost). Since `gcd(11,32)=1`, the mapping is bijective. So no bank conflicts for sequential `tc` across the warp! Great. For `kx=1`: address = `base + tc*11 + 1`. Bank = `(base/4 + tc*11 + 1) % 32`. Still a permutation. So there are no bank conflicts on reads. What about writes? Thread `tc` writes to `shmem[ty*IN_TILE_W + tc + m*31]`. For fixed `m`, address = `base' + tc`. Bank = `(base'/4 + tc) % 32`. This is perfectly coalesced and conflict-free across the 31 threads (a warp is 32 threads, but only 31 are active; the active threads access consecutive banks). So shared memory usage is very efficient. Now, the overall algorithm computes sum of `K*K=121` floats per thread. It's compute-light, memory-bound. The effective memory traffic: - Input: `N*C*H*W` floats read once. - Output: `N*C*OH*OW` floats written once. - Shared memory is internal. No extra global reads/writes. Arithmetic intensity: `1 FLOP` (add) per input element? Actually 121 adds per output element. Total adds = `N*C*OH*OW*121` = `16*64*186*186*121` = about `4.27e9` adds. Total bytes = `N*C*H*W*4 + N*C*OH*OW*4` = `16*64*2048*2048*4 + 16*64*186*186*4` = `17.18 GB + 0.142 GB` = `17.32 GB`. AI = `4.27e9 / 17.32e9` = `0.246` FLOP/byte. This is very memory bound. We need high DRAM bandwidth utilization. The kernel reads each input element exactly once and each thread does 121 adds. The bottleneck is HBM bandwidth. To be fast, the kernel needs good coalescing and high occupancy. Occupancy: block size = 93 threads. Shared memory per block = 1023*4 = 4092 bytes. Register usage: likely small (maybe 24-32). For Hopper (128 KB shared mem per SM), shared memory is not a limit. For 2048 threads per SM, 93 threads per block => max 22 blocks per SM. That's fine. Warp count per block = 3 warps (93 threads = 2 warps + 29 threads? Actually 93/32 = 2.9 warps. 3 warps). With 3 warps per block, 64 warps per SM => max 21 blocks. Still fine. We need to ensure no spills and maximum occupancy. Can we improve performance further? One idea: use vectorized loads. Each thread loads 11 floats. We can load 4 floats + 4 floats + 2 floats + 1 float using float4, but that complicates the indexing and might not be necessary. With 93 threads, and `IN_TILE_W=341`, we have 93 threads * 11 loads = 1023 global loads per `ky` iteration. But these loads are coalesced? Let's see. For a fixed `ty` and `ky`: Thread `tc` loads `in_slice[in_row*W + in_col_base + tc + m*31]`. For a fixed `m`, threads `tc=0..30` load consecutive addresses: `base + tc`. This is perfectly coalesced. There are 11 such coalesced groups per `ky` (for `m=0..10`). So 11 coalesced transactions per warp. Since there are 31 threads active, it's just under a warp. Coalescing may still be good. Total global loads per block per `ky` = 3 rows * 341 floats = 1023 loads. In terms of 128-byte transactions: `341 * 4 = 1364` bytes per row. `1364 / 128 = 10.65` => 11 transactions per row. 3 rows => 33 transactions per `ky`. `K=11` => 363 transactions per block. Total blocks = 380,928. Total global load transactions = 138,377,024. Each is 128 bytes => 17.71 GB loaded. This is close to the theoretical 17.18 GB (some overhead due to not being perfectly aligned to 128B boundaries). Wait, `in_col_base = ow_tile * 341`. Since `341` is not a multiple of 32 floats (128 bytes), the starting address of each row in shared memory may not be aligned to 128 bytes. But the global memory loads are: for `m=0`, `base + tc`, where `base = in_row*W + in_col_base`. `in_col_base = ow_tile * 341`. On the first iteration `ow_tile=0`, `in_col_base=0`, aligned. For `m=1`, address is `base + 31`. `31` floats = 124 bytes, not aligned to 128. So the loads for `m=1` will be slightly misaligned. Still, consecutive threads access consecutive floats, so coalescing into 2 transactions max per 31 threads. To improve alignment, I could pad `IN_TILE_W` to a multiple of 32 (e.g., 352). That would make each `m` group aligned if `in_col_base` is a multiple of 32. But `in_col_base = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W * K = ow_tile * 341`. Not a multiple of 32. Actually, since `W=2048`, and `ow_tile` ranges 0..5, `in_col_base` values are 0, 341, 682, 1023, 1364, 1705. None of these are multiples of 32 except 0. But for `m>0`, adding `m*31` makes it even less aligned. However, still 31 threads loading 11 items each should be quite efficient. Using vector loads could help, but given the small size, it might not be worth the complexity. An alternative kernel design: Use a 1D block of 256 threads, each handling one output pixel. Tiling is less explicit. But that would result in `121` global loads per thread (uncoalesced within the thread, but coalesced across threads for the same `kx` if threads are assigned output columns sequentially). For output columns, the input column is `ow*K + kx`. Threads in a warp handle consecutive `ow`, so `ow*K` increments by `K`. If `K=11`, not a multiple of 32, still okay but memory access pattern is strided by 11 floats across threads. That means for a fixed `kx`, threads access `base + threadIdx.x*11 + kx`. Bank conflicts in shared memory? No shared memory. Global memory coalescing: consecutive threads access addresses separated by 44 bytes. A warp of 32 threads would need `32*44 = 1408` bytes. This would be coalesced into `1408/128 = 11` transactions. For each `kx` (11 values), that's `11*11 = 121` transactions per warp! Very bad. The shared memory tiled approach is much better because it coalesces loads within a warp for each `m` (consecutive addresses). So the SMEM approach is good. Can I improve throughput further by increasing the tile size? Currently `OUT_TILE_W=31` with 93 threads. I could try `OUT_TILE_W=32` with 96 threads (3x32) to fit perfectly into 3 warps. But then `IN_TILE_W = 32*11 = 352`. `shmem = 3*352 = 1056` floats = 4224 bytes. Still small. Total loads per `ky` = 96*11 = 1056. Slightly more. `OW=186` which is not divisible by 32. `186/32 = 5.8125`. So some tiles would need bounds checking or leftover handling, complicating the code. `31` works perfectly (`186 = 6*31`). What about using `float4` loads? Each thread loads 11 floats. If we load a `float4` (16 bytes) and then 3 floats, or two `float4`s overlapping... This gets messy because `IN_TILE_W=341` is not a multiple of 4. And threads are writing to shared memory with stride `m*31`, which is not consecutive for vector loads. Given the memory-bound nature, the kernel should be very close to peak bandwidth if coalescing is good. Let's first see if it compiles and runs correctly with the static linking fix. Wait, can I pass `['-static-libstdc++']` to `extra_cflags`? The C extension compilation involves compiling `main.cpp` and linking it with `cuda.cuda.o` into a shared object. The linker flags for the final `.so` are what matter. In PyTorch's `load_inline`, `extra_ldflags` should be fed to the linker command. Let me try that. Actually, it might be that the build uses `ninja`, and I can see the commands. Let's just add `extra_cflags=['-static-libstdc++']` and `extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++']`. Or maybe we don't need static linking and can just add `['-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0']` to `extra_cflags` to force the old ABI. That might work if the system libstdc++ is old. Actually, the error is `CXXABI_1.3.15 not found`. `CXXABI_1.3.15` corresponds to GCC 13/14. The system libstdc++ is older. Using the old ABI (`_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0`) won't help because it's about the GCC ABI version, not the C++11 ABI. Static linking is the most robust approach here. However, on Linux, static linking of libstdc++ into a shared library is possible but may cause issues with exceptions crossing library boundaries. But for a simple CUDA extension without exceptions, it should be fine. Let me also check if we need to add `['-Wl,--exclude-libs,ALL']` to avoid symbol exports. We can pass `extra_cflags=['-O3', '-static-libstdc++']` and `extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++']`. Let me also try `std='c++17'` (already default) and `-fabi-version=11`... No, static linking should work. Let's rewrite the kernel with the static linking flags.

// 341 for K=11\n constexpr int KK = K * K; // 121 for K=11\n\n __shared__ float shmem[3 * IN_TILE_W]; // 3 * 341 floats (~4KB)\n\n int tc = threadIdx.x; // 0..30\n int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..2\n\n int ow_tile = blockIdx.x;\n int oh_tile = blockIdx.y;\n int nc = blockIdx.z;\n\n const float* in_slice = input + ((size_t)nc * H * W);\n float* out_slice = output + ((size_t)nc * OH * OW);\n\n int in_row_base = oh_tile * 3 * K;\n int in_col_base = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W * K;\n\n float sum = 0.0f;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int ky = 0; kx < K; ++kx) {\n sum += shmem[ty * IN_TILE_W + tc * K + kx];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n\n int oh = oh_tile * 3 + ty;\n int ow = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W + tc;\n out_slice[oh * OW + ow] = sum * (1.0f / static_cast<float>(KK));\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor avgpool2d_forward(torch::Tensor input, int kernel_size, int stride, int padding) {\n int N = input.size(0);\n int C = input.size(1);\n int H = input.size(2);\n int W = input.size(3);\n int OH = (H + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1;\n int OW = (W + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1;\n\n auto output = torch::empty({N, C, OH, OW}, input.options());\n\n bool fast = (kernel_size == 11 && stride == 11 && padding == 0 &&\n OH % 3 == 0 && OW % 31 == 0);\n\n if (fast) {\n dim3 block(31, 3);\n dim3 grid(OW / 31, OH / 3, N * C);\n avgpool2d_kernel<11><<<grid, block>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C, H, W, OH, OW\n );\n } else {\n output = at::avg_pool2d(input, {kernel_size, kernel_size},\n {stride, stride}, {padding, padding},\n false, true);\n }\n return output;\n}\n'''\n\nmodule = load_inline(\n name='avgpool2d_cuda_v3',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['avgpool2d_forward'],\n extra_cflags=['-O3', '-static-libstdc++'],\n extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'],\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, kernel_size: int, stride: int = None, padding: int = 0):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.kernel_size = kernel_size\n self.stride = stride if stride is not None else kernel_size\n self.padding = padding\n\n def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:\n return module.avgpool2d_forward(x, self.kernel_size, self.stride, self.padding)\n" }

Actually, the key insight is: for K=11 and 32 banks, ANY K consecutive elements will cover K banks. For adjacent threads reading K consecutive elements starting at offsets K apart, there will be overlaps because K=11 and 32 are not coprime (gcd(11,32)=1, actually they ARE coprime!). If gcd(K, NUM_BANKS) = 1, then the sequence tc*K mod 32 for tc=0,1,2,... cycles through all 32 banks. But the issue is that within each thread's K-element read, consecutive elements touch K consecutive banks, and the starting banks of adjacent threads differ by K mod 32 = 11. Since K=11 and 32 are coprime, adjacent threads start at banks that are 11 apart. After some threads, the starting banks wrap around and overlap with earlier threads' read ranges. For thread tc, banks accessed: {tc*K, tc*K+1, ..., tc*K+K-1} mod 32. For thread tc+1: {(tc+1)*K, ..., (tc+1)*K+K-1} mod 32. The overlap: (tc+1)*K = tc*K + K. Since K=11, thread tc+1 starts at bank (tc*K+11) mod 32. Thread tc accesses banks tc*K to tc*K+10. Thread tc+1 accesses tc*K+11 to tc*K+21. There is NO overlap between thread tc and tc+1! They access disjoint bank ranges! But what about thread tc and tc+3? tc+3 starts at tc*K+33 = tc*K+1 (mod 32). Thread tc accesses [tc*K, tc*K+10] mod 32 = [b, b+10]. Thread tc+3 accesses [b+1, b+11] mod 32. These overlap on banks b+1 to b+10! So in a warp, threads tc and tc+3 have overlapping bank ranges. With a 32-thread warp, there are many such overlaps. The degree of the conflict for a given bank b: how many threads in the warp access bank b? For bank b and thread tc, bank b is accessed if tc*K <= b <= tc*K+K-1 (mod 32). Since K=11 and threads range 0..31 with warp=32: For bank b, the threads that access it are those where tc*K mod 32 is in [b-K+1, b] mod 32. Since K mod 32 = 11, this is a window of 11 consecutive values of tc*K mod 32. Since K and 32 are coprime, tc*K mod 32 cycles through all values 0..31 as tc goes 0..31. So for each bank b, exactly 11 threads access it (out of 32)! That's an 11-way bank conflict for every bank! This is terrible. But wait, am I right? Let me verify for a specific bank. So even with 31 threads, we have up to 11-way bank conflicts. This is a serious performance issue. How to fix? Change the algorithm to avoid having each thread read K consecutive elements. Approach 1: Use a reduction tree. Load all elements into shared memory. Then have threads compute sums in a tree structure. But for non-overlapping pooling, this is tricky because each output pixel needs a distinct block. Approach 2: Use registers instead of shared memory for the reductions. Load KxK elements into registers and compute the sum. But KxK=121 floats per thread is too many registers. Approach 3: Process one row at a time. Load one input row into shared memory. One more idea: instead of reading from shared memory K times in a row, what if I read all K elements for one output pixel into registers, then sum them with a tree-like reduction? ```cuda float s0 = shmem[base + 0]; float s1 = shmem[base + 1]; ... float s10 = shmem[base + 10]; float part = s0 + s1 + s2 + s3; part += s4 + s5 + s6 + s7; part += s8 + s9 + s10; sum += part; ``` This reduces the dependency chain length. The compiler might do this automatically, but let me make it explicit. Alternatively, use intrinsics like `__fmaf_rn`? No, that's for multiplication, not addition. Actually, the issue with the tree reduction is that all K loads must complete before any adds can start. But in a GPU, loads have high latency (~100 cycles). However, with multiple warps, this latency is hidden. If I interleave loads from different `kx` values with no dependencies, the GPU can issue them in parallel: ```cuda float r0, r1, r2, ..., r10; r0 = shmem[base+0]; r1 = shmem[base+1]; ... r10 = shmem[base+10]; sum += r0; sum += r1; ... sum += r10; ``` The loads can all be issued together, and when they return, the adds execute. This is what happens with full unrolling. Let me try explicit unrolling with ILP (instruction-level parallelism): ```cuda float acc1 = 0.0f, acc2 = 0.0f, acc3 = 0.0f, acc4 = 0.0f; for (int kx = 0; kx < K; kx += 4) { acc1 += shmem[base + kx + 0]; acc2 += shmem[base + kx + 1]; acc3 += shmem[base + kx + 2]; acc4 += shmem[base + kx + 3]; } sum += acc1 + acc2 + acc3 + acc4; ``` Wait, K=11 isn't divisible by 4. I'd need a remainder. But also, this doesn't fix bank conflicts. In fact, it might make them worse because now 4x more loads are in flight simultaneously, potentially increasing contention. Let me try a completely different kernel design. **New Design**: Instead of shared memory for the entire tile, what if I only keep a sliding window and compute partial sums incrementally? For each output row, I need K input rows. For each input row, I compute 1D averages (sum over K consecutive elements). Then I accumulate K of these 1D averages. 1D average per row: for each output column j, compute row_sum[j] = sum(input_row[j*K : j*K+K-1]). Then output[j] = sum over ky of row_sum[ky][j] / K*K. For the 1D average, I can use a warp-level reduction or simple loop. But the key insight: I can compute the 1D average and immediately accumulate it into the output, without storing all row_sums. This way I only need K registers per thread for the running sums. For each output pixel, I have one register `sum`. For each of K input rows: 1. Load K elements for this output pixel. 2. Add their sum to `sum`. This is exactly what my current kernel does! The only difference is whether the K elements are loaded from shared memory or directly. Wait, what if I use a "register cache" for multiple output pixels? If a thread computes multiple output pixels in the same row, it can load a wider window and reuse elements. For example, a thread computing 2 adjacent output pixels (ow and ow+1): - Needs elements [ow*K, ow*K+K-1] and [(ow+1)*K, (ow+1)*K+K-1]. - These are 2K consecutive elements: [ow*K, ow*K+2K-1]. - If K=11, that's 22 elements. I load 22 floats, sum the first 11 for pixel 1, sum the last 11 for pixel 2. This loads 22 floats instead of 22 floats (same total). No savings. What about 3 pixels? 33 elements. Actually, I just realized something: with `#pragma unroll` and the loops fully unrolled, the compiler might be able to schedule the shared memory reads in a way that reduces conflicts. For example, it might group reads from different `kx` values and issue them to different memory banks over multiple cycles. But in a warp, all threads execute the same instruction simultaneously. If all threads execute `LOAD shmem[base+0]`, they all load from their respective `base+0` addresses, which hit the same bank for every 32nd thread. This is a structural conflict that no compiler optimization can fix. Actually, wait. In A100, shared memory is accessed in phases. If multiple threads in the SAME warp access the SAME bank, those accesses are serialized into multiple transactions. But if they access DIFFERENT banks, up to 32 accesses can happen in parallel (one per bank). My analysis showed that for K=11, each bank is accessed by ~11 threads in a 32-thread warp. So for each LOAD instruction, 11 transactions are needed (instead of 1). One potential optimization: since the warp only has 31 threads (not 32), and for some banks fewer than 11 threads access them, the serialization is slightly less. On A100: - Shared memory can issue up to 128 bytes per clock per bank group. - There are 32 banks, each 4 bytes wide. - A warp executes in 4 cycles (8 threads per cycle for regular instructions, but memory instructions might behave differently). Actually, on Ampere, a warp of 32 threads executes in 4 cycles, with 8 threads per cycle. So when a memory instruction is issued, 8 threads are active per cycle. This means bank conflicts only affect threads within the same cycle group! Ah! This is CRUCIAL! I was analyzing conflicts across the entire warp, but if the warp executes in groups of 8, only threads in the SAME group can conflict! On A100, a warp is divided into 4 groups of 8 threads each (or 2 groups of 16 for some operations, but memory usually goes in groups matching the cache line size). Actually, for memory loads, A100 processes 32 threads in a warp over 2 cycles at 16 threads per cycle (since the load/store unit is 16-wide). Or maybe 4 cycles at 8 threads. Let me check: A100 has a 128-byte load/store path. With 32 threads each loading 4 bytes, that's 128 bytes total. So a warp load of 4 bytes per thread can be done in 1 cycle if all accesses are coalesced and conflict-free! Wait, that's for global memory with coalescing. For shared memory, it's different. For shared memory, A100 has 4 banks per "partition", and each partition can handle certain numbers of accesses per cycle. But the key point is: a warp of 32 threads accesses shared memory over multiple cycles, and only threads in the same cycle can conflict for the same bank. If execution is grouped in 8-thread units (as on older architectures), then within each group of 8 threads, I need to check for conflicts. It's simpler: ```cuda __global__ void avgpool2d_direct( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int OH, int OW ) { int ow = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int oh = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int nc = blockIdx.z; if (ow >= OW || oh >= OH) return; int in_row_base = oh * 11; int in_col_base = ow * 11; const float* in_slice = input + ((size_t)nc * H * W); float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ky = 0; ky < 11; ++ky) { #pragma unroll for (int kx = 0; kx < 11; ++kx) { sum += in_slice[(in_row_base + ky) * W + in_col_base + kx]; } } output[((size_t)nc * OH + oh) * OW + ow] = sum * (1.0f / 121.0f); } ``` This is extremely simple. Since 341 isn't divisible by 4, this is tricky, but with OUT_TILE_W=32 and 32 threads, IN_TILE_W=352 which IS divisible by 4. Let me see: If block.x = 32, OUT_TILE_W = 32 effectively (but we only use 31 of them). IN_TILE_W = 352. 352 / 4 = 88 float4. 88 float4 / 32 threads = 2.75 float4 per thread. Not clean. Thread 1 from 176 = 11*16. 176 is divisible by 16? 176/16 = 11. Yes! But 176 is not contiguous with thread 0's data. For coalescing, adjacent threads should load adjacent memory. What if I arrange so that each float4 corresponds to 4 adjacent output pixels' first element? For 4 pixels, their first elements are at 0, 11, 22, 33. These are not contiguous. So can't load them with one float4. What if I transpose the data? First, load K=11 elements for each of 4 output pixels. These are at: Pixel 0: 0, 1, 2, ..., 10 Pixel 1: 11, 12, ..., 21 Pixel 2: 22, 23, ..., 32 Pixel 3: 33, 34, ..., 43 To load these with float4, I'd need groups of 4 contiguous floats. Looking at the above: - Float4 at 0: 0, 1, 2, 3. (Belongs to pixel 0) - Float4 at 4: 4, 5, 6, 7. (Pixel 0) - Float4 at 8: 8, 9, 10, 11. (Mix of pixel 0 and pixel 1) - Float4 at 12: 12, 13, 14, 15. (Mix) ... This gets messy. Some float4s straddle pixel boundaries. In fact, for K=11 and float4 alignment, every 11th pixel starts at a different alignment. 11 not divisible by 4, so pixel boundaries don't align with float4 boundaries. After 4 pixels, we've used 44 floats, which IS divisible by 4. So every 4th pixel boundary aligns with float4! Pixel 0 starts at 0 (align 0). Pixel 1 starts at 11 (align 3). Pixel 2 starts at 22 (align 2). Pixel 3 starts at 33 (align 1). Pixel 4 starts at 44 (align 0). So float4 alignment repeats every 4 pixels! This means if we process 4 pixels at a time, the 4th pixel boundary is float4-aligned. For a block processing 4 pixels, the data is 44 floats = 11 float4. All float4s are aligned! Can I have each thread process 4 pixels? - Block = (8, 3) for 32 pixels. 8 threads * 4 pixels = 32 pixels. - Each thread loads 44 floats = 11 float4. - 8 threads * 11 float4 = 88 float4 total = 352 floats. - But we need 32*11 = 352 floats for 32 pixels. Perfect! Wait, 8 threads, each loading for 4 pixels: Thread 0: pixels 0,1,2,3 → elements 0..43. 11 float4. Thread 1: pixels 4,5,6,7 → elements 44..87. 11 float4. Thread 2: pixels 8,9,10,11 → elements 88..131. 11 float4. ... Thread 7: pixels 28,29,30,31 → elements 308..351. 11 float4. For coalescing: In cycle 0 (warp issue), all 8 threads issue their first float4 load. Thread 0: float4 at 0 Thread 1: float4 at 44 Thread 2: float4 at 88 ... Thread 7: float4 at 308. These are spaced 44 floats = 176 bytes apart. In a warp, 8 threads loading 176 bytes apart means they span 7*176 = 1232 bytes = 9.6 cache lines. But float4 loads need 16-byte alignment. Thread 0 at 0 is aligned. Thread 1 at 176 bytes = 44 floats. 176/16 = 11. So thread 1 is at a 16-byte boundary! Thread 2 at 352 bytes = 22*16. Also aligned! So ALL threads have aligned float4 loads, but they're spaced 176 bytes apart. 176 % 128 = 48, so each thread's first float4 is in a different cache line than the next thread's. Specifically: Thread 0: byte 0 (cache line 0) Thread 1: byte 176 (cache line 1, since 128 < 176 < 256) Thread 2: byte 352 (cache line 2, since 256 < 352 < 384) ... So 8 threads access 8 different cache lines in the first cycle. That's 8 transactions for 8 float4 loads = 32 floats. But if the threads executed the float4 loads simultaneously and independently, the memory system processes them. With 8 cache lines accessed, that's 8 transactions. Each loads 128 bytes, of which 16 bytes are used. So 8*16 = 128 bytes used out of 8*128 = 1024 bytes loaded. 87.5% wasted bandwidth! This is terrible. It would be much better to have all 8 threads load from the SAME cache line. To do that, we need adjacent threads to access adjacent memory. With thread 0 loading 0..15, thread 1 should load 16..31, etc. So the assignment should be: Thread 0 loads elements 0..3 (float4), 4..7, 8..10 + padding? No wait, we want each thread to process 4 pixels eventually. If we load coalesced first, then redistribute: 1. All 8 threads load their chunk of the tile into shared memory. 2. Then each thread reads the 44 elements for its 4 pixels from shared memory. But this is exactly what my original kernel does! The original kernel loads coalesced (thread tc loads elements tc, tc+31, tc+62, ... for K=11 iterations). These are not perfectly coalesced but still better than 176-byte spacing. Let me refine the kernel: ```cuda template <int K> __global__ void avgpool2d_kernel_swizzled( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int OH, int OW ) { constexpr int OUT_TILE_W = 31; constexpr int IN_TILE_W = OUT_TILE_W * K; constexpr int SHMEM_W = IN_TILE_W + (IN_TILE_W >> 5); // 341 + 10 = 351 constexpr float inv_KK = 1.0f / static_cast<float>(K * K); __shared__ float shmem[3 * SHMEM_W]; int tc = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int ow_tile = blockIdx.x; int oh_tile = blockIdx.y; int nc = blockIdx.z; const float* in_slice = input + ((size_t)nc * H * W); float* out_slice = output + ((size_t)nc * OH * OW); int in_row_base = oh_tile * 3 * K; int in_col_base = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W * K; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ky = 0; ky < K; ++ky) { int in_row = in_row_base + ty * K + ky; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) { int col = tc + m * OUT_TILE_W; int addr = ty * SHMEM_W + col + (col >> 5); shmem[addr] = in_slice[in_row * W + in_col_base + col]; } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int kx = 0; kx < K; ++kx) { int col = tc * K + kx; int addr = ty * SHMEM_W + col + (col >> 5); sum += shmem[addr]; } __syncthreads(); } int oh = oh_tile * 3 + ty; int ow = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W + tc; out_slice[oh * OW + ow] = sum * inv_KK; } ``` Wait, I used `3` rows hardcoded. ky < K; ++ky) {\n int in_row = in_row_base + ty * K + ky;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) {\n int col = tc + m * OUT_TILE_W;\n int addr = ty * SHMEM_W + col + (col >> 5);\n shmem[addr] = in_slice[in_row * W + in_col_base + col];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int kx = 0; kx < K; ++kx) {\n int col = tc * K + kx;\n int addr = ty * SHMEM_W + col + (col >> 5);\n sum += shmem[addr];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n\n int oh = oh_tile * 3 + ty;\n int ow = ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W + tc;\n out_slice[oh * OW + ow] = sum * INV_KK;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor avgpool2d_forward(torch::Tensor input, int kernel_size, int stride, int padding) {\n int N = input.size(0);\n int C = input.size(1);\n int H = input.size(2);\n int W = input.size(3);\n int OH = (H + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1;\n int OW = (W + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1;\n\n auto output = torch::empty({N, C, OH, OW}, input.options());\n\n bool fast = (kernel_size == 11 && stride == 11 && padding == 0 &&\n OH % 3 == 0 && OW % 31 == 0);\n\n if (fast) {\n dim3 block(31, 3);\n dim3 grid(OW / 31, OH / 3, N * C);\n avgpool2d_kernel<11><<<grid, block>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C, H, W, OH, OW\n );\n } else {\n output = at::avg_pool2d(input, {kernel_size, kernel_size},\n {stride, stride}, {padding, padding},\n false, true);\n }\n return output;\n}\n'''\n\nmodule = load_inline(\n name='avgpool2d_cuda_v4',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['avgpool2d_forward'],\n extra_cflags=['-O3', '-static-libstdc++'],\n extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'],\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, kernel_size: int, stride: int = None, padding: int = 0):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.kernel_size = kernel_size\n self.stride = stride if stride is not None else kernel_size\n self.padding = padding\n\n def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:\n return module.avgpool2d_forward(x, self.kernel_size, self.stride, self.padding)\n" }

The question is whether the extra shared memory traffic (load from global → store to shared → load from shared → compute) is faster or slower than just (load from global → compute). For compute-bound kernels, shared memory is clearly a win because it enables data reuse. For memory-bound kernels with NO data reuse (each input element read exactly once), shared memory might actually be a loss because of the extra traffic. However, if global memory latency is higher than shared memory latency, staging through shared memory might help because the shared memory loads can happen while the next global memory loads are in flight. But with K=11 per thread and 121 loads, the compiler might not interleave loads well. Actually, let me think about this more carefully. If we load directly from global memory, the compiler sees a sequence of 121 dependent loads (actually independent loads, but they all feed into the same accumulator). The GPU can issue multiple loads in flight, and the memory system can pipeline them. If we use shared memory, the compiler sees: 1. 11 stores to shared memory 2. __syncthreads() 3. 11 loads from shared memory feeding into the accumulator The shared memory loads are much faster (1-2 cycles) than global memory loads (hundreds of cycles). So after the data is in shared memory, the reduction is very fast. With direct global memory, each load takes hundreds of cycles, but they can overlap. With 121 loads and 96 threads per block, there are 96 * 121 = 11,616 memory requests per block. The memory system can handle many requests in parallel. My intuition is that the shared memory approach will be faster because: 1. The reduction from shared memory has much lower latency 2. The data in shared memory stays close to the compute units 3. So for each m iteration, there are no bank conflicts on read or write! The problem is that the global memory accesses are strided by 31 floats = 124 bytes. This means within a warp, threads access addresses 124 bytes apart. A cache line is typically 128 bytes, so each warp touches many cache lines, reducing memory bandwidth efficiency. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncpp_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n\ntorch::Tensor avgpool2d_forward(torch::Tensor input, int kernel_size, int stride, int padding);\n'''\n\ncuda_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n\ntemplate <int K>\n__global__ void avgpool2d_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int N, int C, int H, int W,\n int OH, int OW) {\n constexpr int OUT_TILE_W = 31;\n constexpr int IN_TILE_W = OUT_TILE_W * K; // 341\n constexpr float INV_KK = 1.0f / static_cast<float>(K * K);\n\n __shared__ float shmem[3 * IN_TILE_W];\n\n int tc = threadIdx.x; // 0..30\n int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..2\n int ow_tile = blockIdx.x;\n int oh_tile = blockIdx.y;\n int nc = blockIdx.z; // flattened (n, c)\n\n const float* src = input + ((size_t)nc * H + oh_tile * 3 * K + ty * K) * W\n + ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W * K;\n float* dst = output + ((size_t)nc * OH + oh_tile * 3 + ty) * OW\n + ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W;\n\n float sum = 0.0f;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int ky = 0; ky < K; ++ky) {\n float* sm_row = shmem + ty * IN_TILE_W;\n\n // Each thread loads K contiguous floats from global memory\n #pragma unroll\n for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) {\n sm_row[tc * K + m] = src[ky * W + tc * K + m];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Each thread sums its K contiguous floats from shared memory\n #pragma unroll\n for (int kx = 0; kx < K; ++kx) {\n sum += sm_row[tc * K + kx];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n\n dst[tc] = sum * INV_KK;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor avgpool2d_forward(torch::Tensor input, int kernel_size, int stride, int padding) {\n int N = input.size(0);\n int C = input.size(1);\n int H = input.size(2);\n int W = input.size(3);\n int OH = (H + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1;\n int OW = (W + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1;\n\n auto output = torch::empty({N, C, OH, OW}, input.options());\n\n bool fast = (kernel_size == 11 && stride == 11 && padding == 0 &&\n OH % 3 == 0 && OW % 31 == 0);\n\n if (fast) {\n dim3 block(31, 3);\n dim3 grid(OW / 31, OH / 3, N * C);\n avgpool2d_kernel<11><<<grid, block>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C, H, W, OH, OW\n );\n } else {\n output = at::avg_pool2d(input, {kernel_size, kernel_size},\n {stride, stride}, {padding, padding},\n false, true);\n }\n return output;\n}\n'''\n\nmodule = load_inline(\n name='avgpool2d_cuda_v5',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['avgpool2d_forward'],\n extra_cflags=['-O3', '-static-libstdc++'],\n extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, kernel_size: int, stride: int = None, padding: int = 0):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.kernel_size = kernel_size\n self.stride = stride if stride is not None else kernel_size\n self.padding = padding\n\n def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:\n return module.avgpool2d_forward(x, self.kernel_size, self.stride, self.padding)\n" }

reasoning
The kernel compiles and passes correctness. The disassembly shows: - Memory shared: 41.4% - Memory global: 20.9% - Compute FP32: 20.9% - Others: smaller This looks reasonable. There's a lot of shared memory operations (expected due to the unrolled loops over 11 elements each). Global memory is about 20.9% which is correct for loading input and writing output. However, we might want to further optimize. One important thing: the current kernel uses 31x3 = 93 threads per block and each thread does 11 loads + 11 shared-memory reads per iteration (11 iterations). We're doing a lot of redundant `__syncthreads()` calls (11 of them). But actually, the kernel is already pretty memory-bound since we have very low arithmetic intensity. On H100, the memory bandwidth is 3.35 TB/s. Let's try to make the loads more efficient. One issue: in the current kernel, each thread loads `K` floats with stride 1, but the threads in a warp have a stride of K between their starting addresses. For K=11 and warpSize=32, this means threads 0 and 1 load from addresses 0-10 and 11-21. The address difference is 11 floats = 44 bytes. Since warp threads access addresses 0, 11, 22, ..., 341 floats in their first load. This means the warp's loads are spread across 341 floats = 1364 bytes. This is definitely not fully coalesced, but on modern GPUs with L1 cache and wide memory transactions, it should still be okay since consecutive threads access consecutive 44-byte blocks, which still fall within cache lines. Wait, let me recalculate: - Thread 0 loads addresses 0, 1, 2, ..., 10 - Thread 1 loads addresses 11, 12, ..., 21 - Thread 2 loads addresses 22, 23, ..., 32 - ... These are contiguous blocks of 11 floats. So warp 0 (threads 0-31) loads addresses 0-10, 11-21, ..., 341-351. That spans 352 floats. The L1 cache line on H100 is 128 bytes (32 floats). So the warp loads span 11 cache lines. This is still coalesced in the sense that memory transactions can be issued for each cache line and the threads read from those lines. But since there are 11 cache lines for 31 threads, we're making good use of the memory bus. However, we could improve this by having each thread load elements from the *same* contiguous segment. For instance, have the first 31 threads load 11 floats each sequentially, but with thread 0 doing addresses 0-10, thread 1 doing 11-21, etc. This is actually what we already do! Wait, in the current code: ```cpp for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) { sm_row[tc * K + m] = src[ky * W + tc * K + m]; } ``` This is: thread tc loads `src[ky*W + tc*K + 0]` through `src[ky*W + tc*K + K-1]`. This is contiguous 11 floats per thread, and thread tc+1 starts right after thread tc ends. So a warp loads a contiguous 341-float block. This is perfectly coalesced! All warp threads together access 341 contiguous floats = 1364 bytes. Since memory transactions are 128 bytes, this is about 11 transactions. All threads in the warp participate in each transaction. This is very good coalescing. The issue might be that the compiler is not unrolling the inner loop optimally, but with `#pragma unroll` it should be fine. Let me think about another optimization: we can use float4 loads to reduce the number of instructions. Instead of 11 individual loads, we could load a float4 (4 floats), another float4, and 1 float. But float4 loads require 16-byte alignment. Let's check: `src[ky * W + tc * K + m]` - for tc=0, m=0, the address is `src[ky*W]`. For W=2048, `ky*W` is a multiple of 2048, which is divisible by 4 (2048/4=512). So the base address is aligned. And `tc*K + m` for consecutive m values gives contiguous addresses. The `if` will be evaluated at compile time, and the last iteration won't have the syncthreads. This is a nice optimization. Let me update the kernel with this optimization. Also, I want to make sure the block dimensions produce good occupancy. With 93 threads, the warps per block is 3 (since 93/32 = 2.9, but CUDA rounds up to 3 warps). Actually, CUDA always allocates full warps. So block size 93 means 3 warps (96 threads), but only 93 are active. The last 3 threads in the third warp are inactive. This is fine but slightly wasteful. I could change to 96 threads with 32 outputs per row and 3 rows, but then we'd need OW % 32 == 0. For OW = 186, that's not true. Alternatively, I could use 32 threads per block (1 warp), with each thread computing 3 output rows. So ty goes from 0 to 2 but threadIdx.y = 1. Then I'd have: ``` int ty = threadIdx.x / 31; // or something ``` This might add arithmetic overhead. Let me just stick with 93 threads. Actually, I could rearrange as a 1D block of 93 threads and compute the (tc, ty) manually. This avoids dim2 block overhead but it's essentially the same to the hardware. I'll keep the dim3 block since it's clearer. Let me try the syncthreads optimization and see if it compiles and passes correctness.

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cpp_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>

torch::Tensor avgpool2d_forward(torch::Tensor input, int kernel_size, int stride, int padding);
'''

cuda_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>

template <int K>
__global__ void avgpool2d_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input,
                                 float* __restrict__ output,
                                 int N, int C, int H, int W,
                                 int OH, int OW) {
    constexpr int OUT_TILE_W = 31;
    constexpr int IN_TILE_W  = OUT_TILE_W * K;
    constexpr float INV_KK   = 1.0f / static_cast<float>(K * K);

    __shared__ float shmem[3 * IN_TILE_W];

    int tc = threadIdx.x;
    int ty = threadIdx.y;
    int ow_tile = blockIdx.x;
    int oh_tile = blockIdx.y;
    int nc      = blockIdx.z;

    const float* src = input  + ((size_t)nc * H + oh_tile * 3 * K + ty * K) * W
                               + ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W * K;
    float*       dst = output + ((size_t)nc * OH + oh_tile * 3 + ty) * OW
                               + ow_tile * OUT_TILE_W;

    float sum = 0.0f;

    #pragma unroll
    for (int ky = 0; ky < K; ++ky) {
        float* sm_row = shmem + ty * IN_TILE_W;

        #pragma unroll
        for (int m = 0; m < K; ++m) {
            sm_row[tc * K + m] = src[ky * W + tc * K + m];
        }
        __syncthreads();

        #pragma unroll
        for (int kx = 0; kx < K; ++kx) {
            sum += sm_row[tc * K + kx];
        }
        if (ky < K - 1) __syncthreads();
    }

    dst[tc] = sum * INV_KK;
}

torch::Tensor avgpool2d_forward(torch::Tensor input, int kernel_size, int stride, int padding) {
    int N = input.size(0);
    int C = input.size(1);
    int H = input.size(2);
    int W = input.size(3);
    int OH = (H + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1;
    int OW = (W + 2 * padding - kernel_size) / stride + 1;

    auto output = torch::empty({N, C, OH, OW}, input.options());

    bool fast = (kernel_size == 11 && stride == 11 && padding == 0 &&
                 OH % 3 == 0 && OW % 31 == 0);

    if (fast) {
        dim3 block(31, 3);
        dim3 grid(OW / 31, OH / 3, N * C);
        avgpool2d_kernel<11><<<grid, block>>>(
            input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(),
            N, C, H, W, OH, OW
        );
    } else {
        output = at::avg_pool2d(input, {kernel_size, kernel_size},
                                {stride, stride}, {padding, padding},
                                false, true);
    }
    return output;
}
'''

module = load_inline(
    name='avgpool2d_cuda_v6',
    cpp_sources=cpp_source,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=['avgpool2d_forward'],
    extra_cflags=['-O3', '-static-libstdc++'],
    extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++'],
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],
)

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, kernel_size: int, stride: int = None, padding: int = 0):
        super(ModelNew, self).__init__()
        self.kernel_size = kernel_size
        self.stride = stride if stride is not None else kernel_size
        self.padding = padding

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        return module.avgpool2d_forward(x, self.kernel_size, self.stride, self.padding)
